In an IDA, a host molecule (often a macrocycle such as a calixarene or cyclodextrin) is pre-complexed with an electroactive indicator. When quinine is introduced, it competes with the indicator for binding to the host, displacing the indicator and causing a measurable change in the electrochemical signal (such as current or potential) at the sensor's electrode. This change can be detected using techniques like cyclic voltammetry or differential pulse voltammetry. The specificity and sensitivity of the sensor depend on the host's selectivity for quinine over the indicator and other potential interferents. While most IDA-based quinine sensors reported in the literature are optical, the principles can be adapted to electrochemical platforms, especially by choosing electroactive indicators and suitable host molecules. Calixarenes, for example, are known to form complexes with quinine and can be integrated into electrochemical sensor designs. Such sensors could be valuable for pharmaceutical analysis or food safety applications where quinine quantification is required.", 'key_concepts': ['indicator_displacement_assay(IDA)', 'quinine', The tool analysis highlights 'indicator_displacement_assay(IDA)' and 'quinine' as highly information-rich classes, suggesting that the focus should be on the use of IDA in sensor platforms for quinine detection, with a particular emphasis on electrochemical readouts.", 'hypothetical_answer': "Yes, it is feasible to design electrochemical sensors that employ the Indicator Displacement Assay (IDA) for the detection of quinine, although such systems are less common than optical (colorimetric or fluorescence) IDA sensors. In an IDA, a host molecule (often a macrocycle such as a calixarene or cyclodextrin) is pre-complexed with an electroactive indicator. When quinine is introduced, it competes with the indicator for binding to the host, displacing the indicator and causing a measurable change in the electrochemical signal (such as current or potential) at the sensor's electrode. This change can be detected using techniques like cyclic voltammetry or differential pulse voltammetry. The specificity and sensitivity of the sensor depend on the host's selectivity for quinine over the indicator and other potential interferents. While most IDA-based quinine sensors reported in the literature are optical, the principles can be adapted to electrochemical platforms, especially by choosing electroactive indicators and suitable host molecules. Calixarenes, for example, are known to form complexes with quinine and can be integrated into electrochemical sensor designs. Such sensors could be valuable for pharmaceutical analysis or food safety applications where quinine quantification is required.", 'key_concepts': ['indicator_displacement_assay(IDA)', 'quinine', In an IDA, a host molecule (often a macrocycle such as a calixarene or cyclodextrin) is pre-complexed with an electroactive indicator. When quinine is introduced, it competes with the indicator for binding to the host, displacing the indicator and causing a measurable change in the electrochemical signal (such as current or potential) at the sensor's electrode. This change can be detected using techniques like cyclic voltammetry or differential pulse voltammetry. The specificity and sensitivity of the sensor depend on the host's selectivity for quinine over the indicator and other potential interferents. While most IDA-based quinine sensors reported in the literature are optical, the principles can be adapted to electrochemical platforms, especially by choosing electroactive indicators and suitable host molecules. Calixarenes, for example, are known to form complexes with quinine and can be integrated into electrochemical sensor designs. In an IDA, a host molecule (often a macrocycle such as a calixarene or cyclodextrin) is pre-complexed with an electroactive indicator. When quinine is introduced, it competes with the indicator for binding to the host, displacing the indicator and causing a measurable change in the electrochemical signal (such as current or potential) at the sensor's electrode. This change can be detected using techniques like cyclic voltammetry or differential pulse voltammetry. The specificity and sensitivity of the sensor depend on the host's selectivity for quinine over the indicator and other potential interferents.
